Generated Hypothesis Portfolio

The pivot penalty is causally driven by cognitive constraints on knowledge integration rather than institutional barriers or network effects, as evidenced by the penalty persisting even when controlling for collaboration and funding.
H2 incremental score 0.265; inference inferred
The pivot penalty is weaker for researchers who have higher 'conceptual diversity' in their prior work, as they possess a broader knowledge base that facilitates integration of distant concepts.

Review Walkthrough

  • 1. Provide input: submit a paper PDF, paper URL, arXiv ID, or research guidance through the CLI or an optional chat wrapper.
  • 2. Run agent: the pipeline records every tool step and stores reusable memory.
  • 3. Open this page: explain the dashboard before drilling into details.
  • 4. Show audit logic: GREEN is direct support, YELLOW is partial/inferred, RED is failure or contradiction.

1. Run Summary

This section is the executive checksum for the run. It records the run ID, verifies the most important ordering constraint, and defines the report's audit colors. The key process guarantee is that literature search must occur before hypothesis generation, so the generated idea is grounded in retrieved papers rather than produced from the input PDF alone. Read GREEN/YELLOW/RED as claim-audit labels, not as paper-quality labels.

Run ID: d5defce8-0218-42e1-a433-2b442a06ae87

Literature search happened before hypothesis generation: YES.

GREEN labels require verified references, bibliographic score >= 0.75, direct support, evidence snippets, provenance, and a written reason. The score column in the verification table is bibliographic identity confidence, not support strength. YELLOW means uncertain, partial, or a novel claim that still needs experiment; RED means not found, unsupported, contradicted, or clearly wrong.

Direct evidenceReference identity is verified, evidence explicitly addresses the claim, and the reason explains the support relation.
Indirect evidenceEvidence is relevant background or motivation, but it does not directly prove the full claim.
Background / absentEvidence is too weak, abstract-only, missing, or only useful as context.
Contradictory evidenceEvidence conflicts with the claim or contains explicit contradiction cues.

Four Error Categories

Unsupported ClaimThe conclusion is not supported by any available input material.
OverclaimThe material supports a weaker conclusion, but the generated claim states a stronger one.
Mis-citationThe cited material is topic-related but does not support the specific claim.
ContradictionThe generated claim conflicts with the cited material.

Evidence Source Snapshot

Total evidence records: 46; input-paper evidence: 8; retrieved/external evidence: 38; abstract evidence ratio: 0.696.

2. Input Paper Profiles

This section shows the structured profile extracted from each submitted PDF before any external retrieval or hypothesis generation. A profile contains the detected title, authors, abstract, and high-level source claims. These fields are produced by deterministic PDF parsing and profiling heuristics, with optional LLM assistance only when enabled. They are not treated as final truth: if the title, abstract, author list, or key claims are wrong, later search queries, relatedness filtering, literature-map items, and citation audits should be interpreted cautiously.

Clean profileTitle, authors, abstract, and key claims match the PDF; downstream retrieval is likely aligned.
Partial profileSome fields are missing or noisy; search may still work, but map and hypothesis quality should be reviewed manually.
Bad profileTitle/abstract/key claims are visibly wrong; later sections may be answering the wrong paper and should be treated as unreliable.

The pivot penalty in research

Authors: Ryan Hill, Yian Yin, Carolyn Stein, Xizhao Wang, Dashun Wang, Benjamin F. Jones

Abstract: Scientists and inventors set the direction of their work amid evolving questions, opportunities and challenges, yet the understanding of pivots between research areas and their outcomes remains limited1–5. Theories of creative search highlight the potential benefits of exploration but also emphasize difficulties in moving beyond one’s expertise6–14. Here we introduce a measurement framework to quantify how far researchers move from their existing work, and apply it to millions of papers and patents. We find a pervasive ‘pivot penalty’, in which the impact of new research steeply declines the further a researcher moves from their previous work. The pivot penalty applies nearly universally across science and patenting, and has been growing in magnitude over the past five decades. Larger pivots further exhibit weak engagement with established mixtures of prior knowledge, lower publication success rates and less market impact. Unexpected shocks to the research landscape, which may push researchers away from existing areas or pull them into new ones, further demonstrate substantial pivot penalties, including in the context of the COVID-19 pandemic. The pivot penalty generalizes across fields, career stage, productivity, collaboration and funding contexts, highlighting both the breadth and depth of the adaptive challenge. Overall, the findings point to large and increasing challenges in effectively adapting to new opportunities and threats, with implications for individual researchers, research organizations, science policy and the capacity of science and society as a whole to confront emergent demands.

Key claims:

  • Here we introduce a measurement framework to quantify how far researchers move from their existing work, and apply it to millions of papers and patents.
  • Larger pivots further exhibit weak engagement with established mixtures of prior knowledge, lower publication success rates and less market impact.
  • Unexpected shocks to the research landscape, which may push researchers away from existing areas or pull them into new ones, further demonstrate substantial pivot penalties, including in the context of the COVID-19 pandemic.
  • Scientists and inventors set the direction of their work amid evolving questions, opportunities and challenges, yet the understanding of pivots between research areas and their outcomes remains limited1–5.

3. Paper Index Legend

This section defines the shorthand paper labels used throughout the report. Labels such as P1, P2, ... refer to retrieved or connected papers after deduplication and relatedness filtering; the internal JSON index remains zero-based, so P1 corresponds to JSON index 0. The relation field explains why a paper entered the candidate pool, for example input seed, cited by the input paper, citing the input paper, semantic expansion, or topical search. The relatedness score is the gate score used before map and hypothesis generation; it is not a citation-support score.

Distance 0 / input seedThe submitted paper itself; useful as context but not enough to prove a new idea.
Distance 1-2Cited-by or citing-neighborhood papers; these are stronger candidates for connected literature.
Semantic expansionTitle/abstract-near papers added when citation graph is sparse; useful but weaker than citation-chain evidence.
Low relatednessRejected or marginal papers should not support hypotheses, even if they are real papers.

4. Literature Map Summary

This section turns the connected paper pool into a literature map. A thread is a cluster of papers that share a method, problem setting, or conceptual role. A gap is a missing test, boundary condition, mechanism, or application regime suggested by the connected evidence. A tension records an unresolved contrast, such as two papers making claims that are hard to compare directly or using incompatible assumptions. A cross-domain connection is a possible transfer of an idea from one setting to another. These items are generated from paper titles, abstracts, retrieved snippets, and evidence IDs; they are the immediate inputs to hypothesis generation, not final claims by themselves.

Thread onlyThe literature clusters cleanly, but may not yet imply a new research question.
Gap + evidenceA plausible place to generate a hypothesis; check evidence IDs before trusting it.
TensionA stronger idea source when assumptions, benchmarks, or claims do not line up across papers.
Empty tension/connectionNot a failure by itself; it means the retrieved literature did not expose a clear contrast or transfer opportunity.

Map generation mode: llm. Fallback used: False. Reason: LLM returned a valid literature map with at least one thread and one gap.

Threads

  • T1 Pivot penalty as a universal adaptive challenge: P1 and P2 establish that researchers face a steep decline in impact when pivoting far from prior work, a pattern that holds across fields, career stages, and external shocks like COVID-19. The penalty has grown over five decades and is linked to weak engagement with established knowledge mixtures.
    Evidence: Evidence 4 Evidence 5 Evidence 6 Evidence 7 Evidence 8 Evidence 15 Evidence 16 Evidence 18
  • T2 AI research agents and exploration concentration: P3 shows that AI-generated research ideas are more concentrated near seed literature than human-authored work, suggesting AI may exacerbate the pivot penalty by narrowing exploration.
    Evidence: Evidence 22 Evidence 23 Evidence 24
  • T3 Forecasting breakthroughs and conceptual diffusion: P5 and P6 develop models to predict scientific breakthroughs and conceptual diffusion using network features. P5 finds that structural factors (e.g., Adamic-Adar similarity) drive breakthrough emergence. P6 shows exogenous diffusion is highly predictable, while endogenous reinforcement is not in some fields.
    Evidence: Evidence 29 Evidence 30 Evidence 37 Evidence 38

Gaps

  • G1: No study directly tests whether the pivot penalty can be predicted using concept network features (e.g., Adamic-Adar similarity, degree-based measures) that P5 found predictive of breakthroughs. P1 measures pivot distance but does not use network topology to anticipate penalty severity. Why it matters: If network features predict penalty, researchers could identify less costly pivot directions, and institutions could design support systems for high-risk moves.
    Evidence: Evidence 4 Evidence 30
  • G2: The interaction between AI-assisted ideation (P3) and the pivot penalty (P1) is unexplored. P3 shows AI ideas are concentrated, but whether using AI tools increases or decreases the penalty for human researchers is unknown. Why it matters: As AI becomes integrated in research, understanding its effect on adaptability is crucial for science policy and tool design.
    Evidence: Evidence 4 Evidence 22
  • G3: P1 and P2 document the penalty but do not identify causal mechanisms beyond weak prior knowledge engagement. Whether the penalty stems from cognitive constraints, institutional barriers, or network effects is unresolved. Why it matters: Without mechanistic understanding, interventions to reduce the penalty remain speculative.
    Evidence: Evidence 6 Evidence 16

Tensions

  • X1: P1 and P2 find a pervasive pivot penalty, contradicting theories of creative search that highlight benefits of exploration and outsider advantages. The papers note this tension explicitly.
    Evidence: Evidence 2 Evidence 15
  • X2: P3 shows AI agents narrow exploration, while P5 and P6 suggest that structural features can predict breakthrough recombinations. This raises a tension: if AI narrows exploration, it may miss the very network positions that lead to breakthroughs.
    Evidence: Evidence 22 Evidence 30 Evidence 37

Cross-Domain Connections

  • C1: The concept of 'pivot penalty' from science of science (P1) can be transferred to innovation management and organizational behavior, where similar penalties may apply when firms or teams pivot to new technologies or markets. Rationale: The measurement framework and findings on distance-impact trade-offs are directly applicable to R&D teams and corporate innovation strategies.
    Evidence: Evidence 4
  • C2: Forecasting methods from P5 (concept network dynamics) can be applied to predict the success of researcher pivots, using features like concept similarity and network centrality to estimate pivot penalty severity. Rationale: P5's structural features (Adamic-Adar, degree measures) can be computed for a researcher's concept portfolio to forecast impact after a pivot.
    Evidence: Evidence 29 Evidence 4

5. Selected Hypothesis Portfolio

This section is the short answer: it presents the selected research-idea portfolio. By default the report keeps up to two hypotheses: a primary proposal and, when available, a distinct secondary or more incremental proposal. Each hypothesis is generated from the literature map and connected evidence, then scored for literature support, novelty, feasibility, cross-domain value, risk, obvious-extension penalty, and prior-work similarity. The primary hypothesis is used for novelty search and citation audit below. The secondary hypothesis is retained as a usable alternative, not as proof that it is equally novel or equally well supported.

Primary + high noveltyBest candidate for a new research direction, subject to novelty-search caveats.
Primary + high riskInteresting but fragile; prioritize feasibility and falsification before presenting it as a project.
Incremental secondaryUsable fallback or extension; expected to be less novel but often easier to test.
No evidence IDsIdea is mostly model inference; it should not be treated as source-supported.

Hypothesis generation mode: llm. Fallback used: False. Reason: LLM returned valid hypothesis candidates after binding and review.
Review: {'used': True, 'reason': 'LLM review returned replacement/refined hypotheses.', 'candidate_count': 2}

primary

H1 - The pivot penalty is causally driven by cognitive constraints on knowledge integration rather than institutional barriers or network effects, as evidenced by the penalty persisting even when controlling for collaboration and funding.

Total score: 0.385 Inference: inferred Novelty: 0.6 Feasibility: 0.6 Obvious-extension penalty: 0.4

Why retained: Primary proposal selected for the main novelty search and citation audit. It is the strongest non-incremental candidate when available; otherwise it is the highest-scoring distinct candidate.

Motivation: P1 and P2 document the penalty but do not identify mechanisms. Evidence that penalty generalizes across funding and collaboration contexts (8) suggests cognitive factors may be primary. This hypothesis targets G3.

Novelty position: Proposes a specific causal mechanism (cognitive constraints) for the pivot penalty, moving beyond descriptive findings.

How to test it: Analyze text of high-pivot vs. low-pivot papers for indicators of cognitive integration (e.g., use of bridging terms, citation diversity). Compare penalty magnitude across fields with different institutional flexibility (e.g., lab-based vs. theoretical).

Premise evidence: Evidence 8 Evidence 6

Supporting papers: P1: The pivot penalty in research P2: Adaptability and the Pivot Penalty in Science

Risks:

  • Cognitive constraints are hard to measure directly; institutional barriers may still play a role even if penalty persists across contexts.
incremental

H2 - The pivot penalty is weaker for researchers who have higher 'conceptual diversity' in their prior work, as they possess a broader knowledge base that facilitates integration of distant concepts.

Total score: 0.265 Inference: inferred Novelty: 0.4 Feasibility: 0.9 Obvious-extension penalty: 0.7

Why retained: Retained as a more conservative A+B, transfer, or implementation-adjacent direction that is distinct from the primary proposal. It may be useful follow-up work, but it should not be read as the most novel hypothesis.

Motivation: P1 finds penalty generalizes across productivity and career stage, but does not examine prior diversity. Theories of creative search suggest diverse experience aids recombination. This is a conservative incremental follow-up.

Novelty position: Moderator analysis of pivot penalty using prior diversity, not previously tested.

How to test it: In P1's regression model predicting citation impact, add an interaction term between pivot distance and prior conceptual diversity (measured via concept entropy). Test if interaction is positive (reduced penalty for high diversity).

Premise evidence: Evidence 4 Evidence 8

Supporting papers: P1: The pivot penalty in research

Risks:

  • Diversity may be correlated with other factors like career stage; need careful controls.

6. Hypothesis Quality Audit

This section audits the generated idea itself, not just the references attached to it. The hypothesis is decomposed into components such as idea core, motivation, novelty position, mechanism, feasibility, experiment, and risks. Each component receives a small relevance-ranked evidence bundle when directly relevant evidence exists; otherwise it is labeled as model-inferred or needing review. This avoids padding the hypothesis with weak citations while still showing which parts are grounded in evidence IDs.

Source-stated + GREENThe component restates source text with provenance; it is evidence, not novelty.
Evidence-inspired + YELLOWThe component is motivated by evidence but goes beyond what papers directly prove.
Model-inferred + YELLOWThe component is a generated bridge or mechanism; it needs human review and experiments.
Unsupported / REDThe component lacks evidence, contradicts evidence, or makes an unsupported strong claim.

Summary: Hypothesis quality audit separates what the literature directly says from what the model inferred as a new idea. Generated idea, novelty, and experiment components should usually be YELLOW unless directly restating an established finding.

Components: 14; by status: {'evidence_inspired': 10, 'needs_human_review': 4}; by color: {'yellow': 14}; precision evidence per component: None

Evidence 29literature map T3, literature map C2Explainable Forecasting of Scientific Breakthroughs from Concept Network Dynamicspdf:abstract:p1
literature map T3: context
literature map C2: context
We introduce an explainable machine-learning approach that forecasts the structural precursors of scientific breakthroughs—the emergence and intensification of links between research concepts—by modelling how OpenAlex concept networks evolve over time. Using 59 semantic and topological features, a two-stage LightGBM model jointly predicts the formation and the future weight of concept pairs, adding a regression stage that quantifies expected intensity to prior link-existence forecasts (Gu and Krenn, 2025; Krenn and Zeilinger, 2020). Relative to the state of the art, the approach improves accuracy and explainability at once: comparative validation across four technology and biomedical domains yields ROC–AUC in [0.954, 0.967] at all horizons without re-tuning, exceeding the ∼0.90 of prior models, while every forecast rests on structural, auditable features rather than opaque embeddings.
Evidence 30literature map T3, literature map G1, literature map X2Explainable Forecasting of Scientific Breakthroughs from Concept Network Dynamicspdf:abstract:p1
literature map T3: context
literature map G1: context
literature map X2: context
Feature attribution shows that structural factors— particularly Adamic–Adar similarity and degree-based Hadamard measures—consistently drive accuracy, suggesting that breakthrough-relevant recombinations emerge in tightly connected sub-networks. Two expert-anchored cases, quantum annealing and AI-enabled quantum architectures, show the model surfacing technological convergence consistent with expert expectations. We then outline a three-layer decision architecture—detection, expert translation, institutional integration—that turns these forecasts into evidence-based research strategy and policy, anchored in open data and explainable features.

Case studies reveal that sharp entropy increases coincide with the opening of new conceptual frontiers, while entropy collapses signal technological convergence or paradigm displacement. 7. Hypothesis Novelty Search

This section searches for close prior work after the selected hypothesis is generated. After the primary hypothesis is selected, the system creates novelty-search queries from the hypothesis text, method terms, target problem, and supporting-paper neighborhood, then searches external literature again. The result is a risk assessment: exact/close matches suggest the idea may already exist, near matches suggest the idea needs sharper differentiation, related works provide background, and missing results only mean no close prior work was found by available tools. This section never proves novelty conclusively.

High riskExact or close prior work found; the idea likely needs substantial differentiation.
Medium riskNear work shares method, target problem, or framing; novelty may survive only with a sharper mechanism.
Low riskOnly background-related work found; still not proof of novelty.
Unknown riskSearch failed or evidence is too sparse; do not claim novelty.

Novelty risk: MEDIUM

Conclusion: Similar prior work was found; the idea may need sharper differentiation.

Reason: 1 near match(es) share method, target problem, or abstract-level framing.

8. Cited Reference Integrity Audit

This section audits only the references actually cited by the selected hypothesis atomic claims. It answers a bibliographic question: does the cited reference appear to exist, and do title, authors, year, DOI, venue, or source URL point to the same paper? It deliberately does not judge whether the reference supports the scientific claim; that is handled in the next section. Field status values separate matched/present metadata from missing or uncertain metadata. Missing DOI/year usually means the field was unavailable or not comparable, not automatically that the reference is wrong.

Score is bibliographic identity confidence, not a metadata-completeness percentage. A searched bibliographic record or local input PDF can score highly even when DOI or year is missing, but non-seed references with both DOI and year missing are downgraded to YELLOW.

GREEN integrityReference identity is verified enough for audit use; claim support is still judged separately.
YELLOW integrityReference probably exists but metadata is incomplete or uncertain; support cannot be upgraded to strong confidence.
RED integrityReference is not found or metadata conflicts strongly; related claim-support rows should be RED.
Missing DOI/yearUsually source coverage limitation, not a contradiction, unless other metadata also conflicts.

9. Claim Support Audit

This section audits whether each cited reference supports the specific atomic claim attached to it. Rows in this table are claim-reference pairs, so the same claim ID may appear more than once if multiple references are checked separately. This is stricter than bibliographic integrity: a real paper can be GREEN in the previous section but YELLOW or RED here if it only provides background, supports a weaker claim, discusses a different topic, or lacks usable evidence snippets.

Support type rule: direct means verified reference + explicit evidence for the specific claim; indirect means topic-relevant evidence that motivates but does not prove the claim; background means weak/abstract-only context; absent means no usable evidence or no valid reference.

Error category rule: no_specific_error_detected means this row was not classified as unsupported claim, overclaim, mis-citation, or contradiction. It does not mean the claim is proven; use Color, Support, Support Type, Evidence IDs, and Reason for the final interpretation.

GREEN + directVerified reference and explicit evidence directly support this exact claim.
YELLOW + indirect/backgroundEvidence motivates or contextualizes the claim but does not prove it.
RED + unsupported/absentNo usable evidence, invalid reference, contradiction, or clear unsupported claim.
no_specific_error_detectedNo four-category error was detected; this can still be YELLOW if support is indirect.

10. Evidence Cards

Evidence Cards expand evidence IDs into the human-readable form used throughout the report. Each card shows the source paper, URL or path, location, snippet text, and every claim or map item that reused the evidence. The same snippet can support multiple claims in different ways, so each use has its own support type, error category, and reason. Internal hash IDs are kept only in Appendix B for reproducibility; the numbered Evidence labels are intended for review.

One evidence, one useSimple case: one snippet is used for one claim or map item.
One evidence, multiple usesEach use has its own support type and reason; do not infer one global label for the snippet.
Abstract locationUseful but shallow; direct support from abstract alone should be interpreted cautiously.
PDF/full-text locationUsually stronger provenance because the snippet comes from a section or page-level source.

Evidence 29

Source paper: Explainable Forecasting of Scientific Breakthroughs from Concept Network Dynamics

Source URL / path: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03864.pdf

Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Used by: literature map T3, literature map C2

Used bySupport typeError categoryReason
literature map T3contextno_specific_error_detected
literature map C2contextno_specific_error_detected
We introduce an explainable machine-learning approach that forecasts the structural precursors of scientific breakthroughs—the emergence and intensification of links between research concepts—by modelling how OpenAlex concept networks evolve over time. Using 59 semantic and topological features, a two-stage LightGBM model jointly predicts the formation and the future weight of concept pairs, adding a regression stage that quantifies expected intensity to prior link-existence forecasts (Gu and Krenn, 2025; Krenn and Zeilinger, 2020). Relative to the state of the art, the approach improves accuracy and explainability at once: comparative validation across four technology and biomedical domains yields ROC–AUC in [0.954, 0.967] at all horizons without re-tuning, exceeding the ∼0.90 of prior models, while every forecast rests on structural, auditable features rather than opaque embeddings.

Evidence 30

Source paper: Explainable Forecasting of Scientific Breakthroughs from Concept Network Dynamics

Source URL / path: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03864.pdf

literature map X2contextno_specific_error_detected
Feature attribution shows that structural factors— particularly Adamic–Adar similarity and degree-based Hadamard measures—consistently drive accuracy, suggesting that breakthrough-relevant recombinations emerge in tightly connected sub-networks. Two expert-anchored cases, quantum annealing and AI-enabled quantum architectures, show the model surfacing technological convergence consistent with expert expectations. We then outline a three-layer decision architecture—detection, expert translation, institutional integration—that turns these forecasts into evidence-based research strategy and policy, anchored in open data and explainable features.

Evidence 37

Source paper: Forecasting Conceptual Diffusion in Science: The Case of Quantum Computing

Source URL / path: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03919.pdf

Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Used by: literature map T3, literature map X2

literature map T3contextno_specific_error_detected
literature map X2contextno_specific_error_detected
In contrast, exogenous diffusion and entropy are strongly predictable (𝑅2 up to 0.78) and are driven by upstream heterogeneity, citation breadth, and distributional dispersion, as shown by SHAP analyses; replications on robotics, advanced materials, and neuro implants confirm that exogenous diffusion remains the top-ranked target across fields (𝑅2 test ≈0.60–0.87), while endogenous predictability rises markedly in neuro implants (𝑅2 test = 0.83), indicating that the quantum-computing asymmetry does not generalise uniformly. Case studies reveal that sharp entropy increases coincide with the opening of new conceptual frontiers, while entropy collapses signal technological convergence or paradigm displacement. These results demonstrate that conceptual diffusion is governed by stable structural regularities embedded in semantic and citation environments.

11. Controlled Citation Audit Cases

This section is a controlled capability demonstration, separate from the selected hypothesis. The system constructs one direct-support case, one uncertain or inferred case, and one deliberately wrong or nonexistent-reference case. These probes show whether the audit machinery can produce GREEN, YELLOW, and RED under known conditions. They should not be interpreted as extra scientific claims made by the hypothesis generator.

GREEN controlled caseKnown direct support case; should have evidence and pass as GREEN.
YELLOW controlled caseGenerated or inferred claim; should not be forced to GREEN.
RED controlled caseFake reference, contradiction, or unsupported absolute claim; should never become GREEN.
Failing controlled caseIndicates an audit rule regression rather than a scientific finding.

14. Memory Influence Summary

This section explains the role of persistent SQLite memory in the current run. Memory can store prior papers, references, hypotheses, evidence records, rejected evidence, and verification outcomes. Retrieval does not automatically become evidence: previous memory may influence query planning, flag duplicate-risk, or warn about previously rejected material, but direct verification is reused only when the current claim, reference, evidence, and rule version match. If memory was retrieved but did not materially affect the run, the report says so explicitly.

Retrieved + materially alteredMemory changed query planning, duplicate-risk assessment, or direct verification reuse.
Retrieved + not usedMemory was available but treated as context only; this is acceptable and should be reported honestly.
Duplicate risk highA similar previous hypothesis exists; novelty should be interpreted cautiously.
Negative memoryPreviously rejected snippets or noisy candidates are warning signals, never scientific counter-evidence by themselves.

Memory influenced query planning/context through same-scope paper hits or memory-derived queries.

Memory scope: input-fb4a6c1339457c94; mode: real_llm; version: memory-v2

Duplicate risk: medium

Materially altered this run: True

Negative Memory

These are previous rejected evidence snippets or failed candidates. They are warning-only memories: useful for avoiding repeated retrieval mistakes, identifying noisy sources, or explaining why a candidate was ignored, but never evidence that a scientific claim is false. Scientific support and contradiction must still come from current, inspectable evidence snippets.

Reflection findings are not hidden; they are reported as limitations or recommended next actions.

Low severityInformational limitation; useful for review but not necessarily blocking.

16. Warnings and Limitations

This section collects automatic warnings and limitations detected during the run. Warnings are not automatic failures, but they mark places where a human reviewer should be cautious: shallow evidence, few retrieved papers, all-YELLOW claim support, high novelty risk, weak memory influence, API fallback, noisy reference extraction, or generated claims that remain mostly inferred.

No warningsThe automated checks found no obvious limitation, but human review is still required.
Evidence warningsSupport may be shallow, abstract-only, or missing full-text provenance.
Retrieval warningsFew connected papers, API fallback, or noisy search means the map may be incomplete.
Hypothesis warningsThe final idea may be mostly inferred, low-scoring, or duplicate-risk.
  • All selected-hypothesis claim support results are YELLOW; the generated idea is mostly inferred and needs new experiments.
  • Some selected hypotheses are grounded only in input-paper evidence (H1, H2); treat them as within-paper extrapolations unless external supporting papers are added.
  • Generated hypothesis support is mainly indirect, background, or absent.

Real API mode depends on public endpoints, available abstracts/full text, and external service coverage. Absence of retrieved close prior work is not proof of novelty.

10(E-c8617f2a68a4)full_text_pdfinput_pdfThe pivot penalty in researchpdf:body:p10.95Jones1,2,3,9 ✉ Scientists and inventors set the direction of their work amid evolving questions, opportunities and challenges, yet the understanding of pivots between research areas and their outcomes remains limited1–5. Here we introduce a measurement framework to quantify how far researchers move from their existing work, and apply it to millions of papers and patents. We find a pervasive ‘pivot penalty’, in which the impact of new research steeply declines the further a researcher moves from their previous work.
11(E-8f563fbceeb3)full_text_pdfinput_pdfThe pivot penalty in researchpdf:method:p60.95Thus, scientists who ventured further from their previous work to write COVID-19 papers were not immune to the pivot penalty; rather, they produced research with less impact, on average, relative to low-pivot COVID-19 papers. Importantly, the pivot penalty was sufficiently steep that the COVID-19 impact premium was mostly offset by the unusually large pivots associated with COVID-19 research. For example, the upper 45% of COVID-19 papers by pivot size had lower average journal placement than did non-COVID papers with median or smaller pivot size. These findings underscore a central tension for individual researchers and the adaptability of science in response to external opportunities: working in a high-demand area has value, but pivoting leads to penalties that offset it.
12(E-af9f66629261)full_text_pdfinput_pdfThe pivot penalty in researchpdf:discussion:p60.95d, Comparing COVID-19 and non-COVID-19 papers in each field in 2020, unusually large pivots have been a universal feature of COVID-19 research. Comparing at the median pivot sizes (dashed lines), the COVID-19 impact premium was substantially offset by the pivot penalty, given its larger median pivot size. f,g,h, Engaging new collaborators was particularly common for COVID-19 researchers, who worked with new collaborators to an unusual degree compared with their own previous work, their other 2020 publications and with control scientists (f).
13(E-a6ffaf6455cc)full_text_pdfinput_pdfThe pivot penalty in researchpdf:body:p10.95We find a pervasive ‘pivot penalty’, in which the impact of new research steeply declines the further a researcher moves from their previous work. The pivot penalty applies nearly universally across science and patenting, and has been growing in magnitude over the past five decades. Larger pivots further exhibit weak engagement with established mixtures of prior knowledge, lower publication success rates and less market impact.

29(E-82955832d82c)full_text_pdfcitation_graphExplainable Forecasting of Scientific Breakthroughs from Concept Network Dynamicspdf:abstract:p10.95We introduce an explainable machine-learning approach that forecasts the structural precursors of scientific breakthroughs—the emergence and intensification of links between research concepts—by modelling how OpenAlex concept networks evolve over time. Using 59 semantic and topological features, a two-stage LightGBM model jointly predicts the formation and the future weight of concept pairs, adding a regression stage that quantifies expected intensity to prior link-existence forecasts (Gu and Krenn, 2025; Krenn and Zeilinger, 2020). Relative to the state of the art, the approach improves accuracy and explainability at once: comparative validation across four technology and biomedical domains yields ROC–AUC in [0.954, 0.967] at all horizons without re-tuning, exceeding the ∼0.90 of prior models, while every forecast rests on structural, auditable features rather than opaque embeddings.
30(E-c715cfabfda2)full_text_pdfcitation_graphExplainable Forecasting of Scientific Breakthroughs from Concept Network Dynamicspdf:abstract:p10.947Feature attribution shows that structural factors— particularly Adamic–Adar similarity and degree-based Hadamard measures—consistently drive accuracy, suggesting that breakthrough-relevant recombinations emerge in tightly connected sub-networks. Two expert-anchored cases, quantum annealing and AI-enabled quantum architectures, show the model surfacing technological convergence consistent with expert expectations. We then outline a three-layer decision architecture—detection, expert translation, institutional integration—that turns these forecasts into evidence-based research strategy and policy, anchored in open data and explainable features.

35(E-376053df6b2b)full_text_pdfcitation_graphForecasting Conceptual Diffusion in Science: The Case of Quantum Computingpdf:background:p30.95Likewise, adoption diversity, i.e., the breadth of downstream uptake, signals whether ideas diffuse beyond their local communities and reshape multiple research areas (Shi and Evans, 2023; Veugelers and Wang, 2019). To study these processes, researchers increasingly model science at the semantic level, using concept co-occurrence networks in which nodes represent scientific concepts and edges mark their joint appearance in publications. These networks capture the combinatorial substrate of scientific discovery and reveal temporal patterns of emergence, consolidation, and obsolescence (Chen, 2017; Kuhn, 1997; Salatino et al., 2017).

41(E-73ef08ab67ed)full_text_pdfcitation_graphMerit or networks? What decides where research is publishedpdf:abstract:p10.95Using economics as a case study, we combine this text-legible idea-quality score with an execution-quality rubric, a connection index, an author-ability index, and an off-the-shelf language-model text score to estimate a five-input production function for journal placement across 6,208 economics working papers. Connections work through two additive channels: connected authors write papers that score higher, and at equal scores their papers are still more likely to place better. Connections raise the odds of every rung without making the apex the typical outcome for ordinary ideas, and even the highest-scoring papers face real friction reaching the visible journal ladder.

43(E-1b89048d3cfe)full_text_pdfcitation_graphMerit or networks? What decides where research is publishedpdf:abstract:p10.95The question is universal to prestige-driven science, yet it has resisted decades of study because a paper’s quality could not be gauged ahead of its publication fate without using that fate as the yardstick. We break this constraint by measuring a paper’s idea quality directly from its text, before publication, using a disciplinetrained evaluator that scores the idea without seeing author names or outcomes. Using economics as a case study, we combine this text-legible idea-quality score with an execution-quality rubric, a connection index, an author-ability index, and an off-the-shelf language-model text score to estimate a five-input production function for journal placement across 6,208 economics working papers.
44(E-9ad77e617be7)full_text_pdfcitation_graphMerit or networks? What decides where research is publishedpdf:discussion:p160.912For three decades the question of whether good ideas or good connections decide where scientific work lands resisted answer, because quality could not be measured before the outcome without using the outcome as the yardstick. An ex-ante, name-blind, text-derived measure of idea quality removes that constraint, and with it we estimate a five-input production function for journal placement across a large cohort of economics working papers. Execution sets a meritocratic floor and is the largest input on the broad margins; text-legible idea quality grades the ladder; and connections set a favoritism ceiling that bites near the apex through two additive channels: connected authors write higher-scoring papers, and at equal scores they still place about one rung higher.
45(E-6419a2468d5e)retrieved_abstractcitation_graphMerit or networks? What decides where research is publishedabstract0.898Does scientific publishing reward the quality of ideas or the advantage of connections?
46(E-8f7119d59723)full_text_pdfcitation_graphMerit or networks? What decides where research is publishedpdf:introduction:p20.89Researchers have therefore had to choose between a contaminated pre-treatment control and a circular post-treatment one, leaving a literature rich in evidence yet inconclusive on the central question. Studies of gatekeeper ties show that connected authors publish more, and in better venues, and that connections can shape the treatment a paper receives in review (3–7). Several of these works do try to separate favoritism from quality, but only with ex-post citations, which are themselves shaped by author status, publication venue, and a documented bias against novel work (8–10), and so cannot hold pre-publication quality fixed.

CL3 - YELLOW

The pivot penalty applies nearly universally across science and patenting, and has been growing in magnitude over the past five decades. Larger pivots further exhibit weak engagement with established mixtures of prior knowledge, lower publication success rates and less market impact. Unexpected shocks to the research landscape, which may push researchers away from existing areas or pull them into new ones, further demonstrate substantial pivot penalties, including in the context of the COVID-19 pandemic.

Evidence ID: 54 (E-bb8fe5592f9c); Source: input_pdf; Location: pdf:body:p1; Paper: The pivot penalty in research; URL: D:\WeChat Files\wxid_6nut6ell2yad22\FileStorage\File\2026-06\s41586-025-09048-1(1).pdf; Confidence: 0.95
Jones1,2,3,9 ✉ Scientists and inventors set the direction of their work amid evolving questions, opportunities and challenges, yet the understanding of pivots between research areas and their outcomes remains limited1–5. Here we introduce a measurement framework to quantify how far researchers move from their existing work, and apply it to millions of papers and patents. We find a pervasive ‘pivot penalty’, in which the impact of new research steeply declines the further a researcher moves from their previous work.

Pivoting in response to external events The pivot penalty indicates that larger pivots are strongly associated with lower impact. However, the research landscape is constantly shifting, and researchers must weigh opportunities nearer to and further from their current research streams. To further probe pivoting behaviour and the pivot penalty, we considered external events that may provoke researchers to pivot.
